<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Vaccine</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Pathogen-derived preparation that provides acquired immunity to an infectious disease</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Vaccine_(disambiguation)" title="Vaccine (disambiguation)">Vaccine (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p>A <b>vaccine</b> is a biological <a href="/wiki/Dosage_form" title="Dosage form">preparation</a> that provides active <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acquired_immunity" title="Acquired immunity">acquired immunity</a> to a particular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">infectious disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iac39_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-iac39-1">[1]</a></sup> A vaccine typically contains an agent that resembles a disease-causing microorganism and is often made from weakened or <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">killed forms of the microbe, its toxins, or one of its surface proteins</a>. The agent stimulates the body's <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> to recognize the agent as a threat, destroy it, and to further recognize and destroy any of the microorganisms associated with that agent that it may encounter in the future. Vaccines can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophylaxis" title="Prophylaxis">prophylactic</a> (to prevent or ameliorate the effects of a future <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infection</a> by a natural or "wild" <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogen</a>), or <a href="/wiki/Therapeutic_vaccines" title="Therapeutic vaccines">therapeutic</a> (to fight a disease that has already occurred, such as <a href="/wiki/Cancer_vaccine" title="Cancer vaccine">cancer</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26214521_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26214521-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26861670_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26861670-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24748633_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24748633-5">[5]</a></sup> Some vaccines offer full <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sterilizing_immunity" title="Sterilizing immunity">sterilizing immunity</a>, in which infection is prevented completely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The administration of vaccines is called <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a>. Vaccination is the most effective method of preventing infectious diseases;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> widespread immunity due to vaccination is largely responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Eradication_of_infectious_diseases" title="Eradication of infectious diseases">worldwide eradication</a> of <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> and the restriction of diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Polio" title="Polio">polio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tetanus" title="Tetanus">tetanus</a> from much of the world. The effectiveness of vaccination has been widely studied and verified;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20201120_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20201120-8">[8]</a></sup> for example, vaccines that have proven effective include the <a href="/wiki/Influenza_vaccine" title="Influenza vaccine">influenza vaccine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/HPV_vaccine" title="HPV vaccine">HPV vaccine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chickenpox_vaccine" title="Chickenpox vaccine">chickenpox vaccine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) reports that licensed vaccines are currently available for twenty-five different <a href="/wiki/Vaccine-preventable_diseases" title="Vaccine-preventable diseases">preventable infections</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The folk practice of <a href="/wiki/Inoculation" title="Inoculation">inoculation</a> against <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> was brought from <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> to Britain in 1721 by <a href="/wiki/Lady_Mary_Wortley_Montagu" title="Lady Mary Wortley Montagu">Lady Mary Wortley Montagu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montagu's_bold_experiment_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Montagu's_bold_experiment-13">[13]</a></sup>
The terms <i>vaccine</i> and <i>vaccination</i> are derived from <i>Variolae vaccinae</i> (smallpox of the cow), the term devised by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Jenner" title="Edward Jenner">Edward Jenner</a> (who both developed the concept of vaccines and created the first vaccine) to denote <a href="/wiki/Cowpox" title="Cowpox">cowpox</a>. He used the phrase in 1798 for the long title of his <i>Inquiry into the Variolae vaccinae Known as the Cow Pox</i>, in which he described the protective effect of cowpox against smallpox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baxby1999_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baxby1999-14">[14]</a></sup> In 1881, to honor Jenner, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> proposed that the terms should be extended to cover the new protective inoculations then being developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pasteur1881_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pasteur1881-15">[15]</a></sup> The science of vaccine development and production is termed <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/vaccinology" title="wikt:vaccinology">vaccinology</a></i>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vaccination-introduction-and-cases-or-deaths-scaled.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1827" decoding="async" height="560" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Vaccination-introduction-and-cases-or-deaths-scaled.jpg/400px-Vaccination-introduction-and-cases-or-deaths-scaled.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Vaccination-introduction-and-cases-or-deaths-scaled.jpg/600px-Vaccination-introduction-and-cases-or-deaths-scaled.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Vaccination-introduction-and-cases-or-deaths-scaled.jpg/800px-Vaccination-introduction-and-cases-or-deaths-scaled.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vaccination-introduction-and-cases-or-deaths-scaled.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Infectious diseases before and after a vaccine was introduced. Vaccinations have a direct effect on the diminishment of the number of cases and contributes indirectly to a diminishment of the number of deaths.</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RougeoleDP.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="652" decoding="async" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/RougeoleDP.jpg/220px-RougeoleDP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/RougeoleDP.jpg/330px-RougeoleDP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/RougeoleDP.jpg/440px-RougeoleDP.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RougeoleDP.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A child with measles, a vaccine-preventable disease<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>There is overwhelming scientific consensus that vaccines are a very safe and effective way to fight and eradicate infectious diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> recognizes vaccine agents as foreign, destroys them, and "remembers" them. When the <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulent</a> version of an agent is encountered, the body recognizes the protein coat on the virus, and thus is prepared to respond, by first neutralizing the target agent before it can enter cells, and secondly by recognizing and destroying infected cells before that agent can multiply to vast numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mak_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mak-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clem_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clem-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Limitations to their effectiveness, nevertheless, exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19393917_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19393917-23">[23]</a></sup> Sometimes, protection fails for vaccine-related reasons such as failures in vaccine attenuation, vaccination regimens or administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wied_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-wied-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Failure may also occur for host-related reasons if the host's immune system does not respond adequately or at all. Host-related lack of response occurs in an estimated 2-10% of individuals, due to factors including genetics, immune status, age, health and nutritional status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wied_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-wied-24">[24]</a></sup> One type of <a href="/wiki/Primary_immunodeficiency" title="Primary immunodeficiency">primary immunodeficiency</a> disorder resulting in genetic failure is <a href="/wiki/X-linked_agammaglobulinemia" title="X-linked agammaglobulinemia">X-linked agammaglobulinemia</a>, in which the absence of an enzyme essential for <a href="/wiki/B_cell" title="B cell">B cell</a> development prevents the host's immune system from generating <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reda_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reda-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Host–pathogen interactions and responses to infection are dynamic processes involving multiple pathways in the immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jo_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jo-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janeway_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway-28">[28]</a></sup> A host does not develop antibodies instantaneously: while the body's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Innate_immunity" title="Innate immunity">innate immunity</a> may be activated in as little as twelve hours, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adaptive_immunity" title="Adaptive immunity">adaptive immunity</a> can take 1–2 weeks to fully develop. During that time, the host can still become infected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Once antibodies are produced, they may promote immunity in any of several ways, depending on the class of antibodies involved. Their success in clearing or inactivating a pathogen will depend on the amount of antibodies produced and on the extent to which those antibodies are effective at countering the strain of the pathogen involved, since different strains may be differently susceptible to a given immune reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janeway_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway-28">[28]</a></sup> 
In some cases vaccines may result in partial immune protection (in which immunity is less than 100% effective but still reduces risk of infection) or in temporary immune protection (in which immunity wanes over time) rather than full or permanent immunity. They can still raise the reinfection threshold for the population as a whole and make a substantial impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gomes_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gomes-30">[30]</a></sup> They can also mitigate the severity of infection, resulting in a lower <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality rate</a>, lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morbidity" title="Morbidity">morbidity</a>, faster recovery from illness, and a wide range of other effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonanni_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bonanni-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanciu_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanciu-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Those who are older often display less of a response than those who are younger, a pattern known as <a href="/wiki/Immunosenescence" title="Immunosenescence">Immunosenescence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frasca_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frasca-33">[33]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Immunologic_adjuvant" title="Immunologic adjuvant">Adjuvants</a> commonly are used to boost immune response, particularly for older people whose immune response to a simple vaccine may have weakened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neighmond2010_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-neighmond2010-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Vaccine_efficacy" title="Vaccine efficacy">efficacy</a> or performance of the vaccine is dependent on several factors:
</p>
<ul><li>the disease itself (for some diseases vaccination performs better than for others)</li>
<li>the strain of vaccine (some vaccines are specific to, or at least most effective against, particular strains of the disease)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10435956_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10435956-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li>whether the <a href="/wiki/Vaccination_schedule" title="Vaccination schedule">vaccination schedule</a> has been properly observed.</li>
<li>idiosyncratic response to vaccination; some individuals are "non-responders" to certain vaccines, meaning that they do not generate antibodies even after being vaccinated correctly.</li>
<li>assorted factors such as ethnicity, age, or genetic predisposition.</li></ul>
<p>If a vaccinated individual does develop the disease vaccinated against (<a href="/wiki/Breakthrough_infection" title="Breakthrough infection">breakthrough infection</a>), the disease is likely to be less virulent than in unvaccinated cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12955637_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12955637-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Important considerations in an effective vaccination program:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>careful modeling to anticipate the effect that an immunization campaign will have on the epidemiology of the disease in the medium to long term</li>
<li>ongoing surveillance for the relevant disease following introduction of a new vaccine</li>
<li>maintenance of high immunization rates, even when a disease has become rare</li></ol>
<p>In 1958, there were 763,094 cases of measles in the United States; 552 deaths resulted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15106120_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15106120-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18463608_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18463608-39">[39]</a></sup> After the introduction of new vaccines, the number of cases dropped to fewer than 150 per year (median of 56).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18463608_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18463608-39">[39]</a></sup> In early 2008, there were 64 suspected cases of measles. Fifty-four of those infections were associated with importation from another country, although only thirteen percent were actually acquired outside the United States; 63 of the 64 individuals either had never been vaccinated against measles or were uncertain whether they had been vaccinated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18463608_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18463608-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vaccines led to the eradication of <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, one of the most contagious and deadly diseases in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Other diseases such as rubella, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poliomyelitis" title="Poliomyelitis">polio</a>, measles, mumps, <a href="/wiki/Chickenpox" title="Chickenpox">chickenpox</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid</a> are nowhere near as common as they were a hundred years ago thanks to widespread vaccination programs. As long as the vast majority of people are vaccinated, it is much more difficult for an outbreak of disease to occur, let alone spread. This effect is called <a href="/wiki/Herd_immunity" title="Herd immunity">herd immunity</a>. Polio, which is transmitted only among humans, is targeted by an extensive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poliomyelitis_eradication" title="Poliomyelitis eradication">eradication campaign</a> that has seen endemic polio restricted to only parts of three countries (Afghanistan, Nigeria, and Pakistan).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eradication1_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-eradication1-41">[41]</a></sup> However, the difficulty of reaching all children, cultural misunderstandings, and <a href="/wiki/Disinformation" title="Disinformation">disinformation</a> have caused the anticipated eradication date to be missed several times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vaccines also help prevent the development of antibiotic resistance. For example, by greatly reducing the incidence of pneumonia caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Streptococcus_pneumoniae" title="Streptococcus pneumoniae">Streptococcus pneumoniae</a></i>, vaccine programs have greatly reduced the prevalence of infections resistant to penicillin or other first-line antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>The measles vaccine is estimated to prevent a million deaths every year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sullivan2005_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-sullivan2005-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adverse_effects">Adverse effects</span></h3>
<p>Vaccinations given to children, adolescents, or adults are generally safe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mag2014_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mag2014-49">[49]</a></sup> Adverse effects, if any, are generally mild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2013_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2013-50">[50]</a></sup> The rate of side effects depends on the vaccine in question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2013_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2013-50">[50]</a></sup> Some common side effects include fever, pain around the injection site, and muscle aches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2013_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2013-50">[50]</a></sup> Additionally, some individuals may be allergic to ingredients in the vaccine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/MMR_vaccine" title="MMR vaccine">MMR vaccine</a> is rarely associated with <a href="/wiki/Febrile_seizure" title="Febrile seizure">febrile seizures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mag2014_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mag2014-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Host-("vaccinee")-related determinants that render a person susceptible to infection, such as <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, health status (underlying disease, nutrition, pregnancy, <a href="/wiki/Hypersensitivity" title="Hypersensitivity">sensitivities</a> or <a href="/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">allergies</a>), <a href="/wiki/Immunocompetence" title="Immunocompetence">immune competence</a>, age, and <a href="/wiki/Economic_impact_of_the_COVID-19_pandemic" title="Economic impact of the COVID-19 pandemic">economic impact</a> or <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_psychological_environment" title="Synthetic psychological environment">cultural environment</a> can be primary or secondary factors affecting the severity of infection and response to a vaccine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wied_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-wied-24">[24]</a></sup> Elderly (above age 60), <a href="/wiki/Type_I_hypersensitivity" title="Type I hypersensitivity">allergen-hypersensitive</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obese</a> people have susceptibility to compromised <a href="/wiki/Immunogenicity" title="Immunogenicity">immunogenicity</a>, which prevents or inhibits vaccine effectiveness, possibly requiring separate vaccine technologies for these specific populations or repetitive <a href="/wiki/Booster_dose" title="Booster dose">booster vaccinations</a> to limit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transmission_(medicine)" title="Transmission (medicine)">virus transmission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wied_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-wied-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Severe side effects are extremely rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mag2014_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mag2014-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Varicella_vaccine" title="Varicella vaccine">Varicella vaccine</a> is rarely associated with complications in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunodeficient" title="Immunodeficient">immunodeficient</a> individuals, and <a href="/wiki/Rotavirus_vaccine" title="Rotavirus vaccine">rotavirus vaccines</a> are moderately associated with <a href="/wiki/Intussusception_(medical_disorder)" title="Intussusception (medical disorder)">intussusception</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mag2014_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mag2014-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>At least 19 countries have no-fault compensation programs to provide compensation for those with severe adverse effects of vaccination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The United States' program is known as the <a href="/wiki/National_Childhood_Vaccine_Injury_Act" title="National Childhood Vaccine Injury Act">National Childhood Vaccine Injury Act</a>, and the United Kingdom employs the <a href="/wiki/Vaccine_Damage_Payment" title="Vaccine Damage Payment">Vaccine Damage Payment</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topics_Race_for_a_COVID-19_vaccine_01_12Jan2021.jpg"><img alt='Illustration with the text "There are three main approaches to making a vaccine: Using a whole virus or bacterium Parts that trigger the immune system Just the genetic material."' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2377" data-file-width="3961" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topics_Race_for_a_COVID-19_vaccine_01_12Jan2021.jpg/220px-WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topics_Race_for_a_COVID-19_vaccine_01_12Jan2021.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topics_Race_for_a_COVID-19_vaccine_01_12Jan2021.jpg/330px-WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topics_Race_for_a_COVID-19_vaccine_01_12Jan2021.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topics_Race_for_a_COVID-19_vaccine_01_12Jan2021.jpg/440px-WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topics_Race_for_a_COVID-19_vaccine_01_12Jan2021.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topics_Race_for_a_COVID-19_vaccine_01_12Jan2021.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p>Vaccines typically contain attenuated, inactivated or dead organisms or purified products derived from them. There are several types of vaccines in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> These represent different strategies used to try to reduce the risk of illness while retaining the ability to induce a beneficial immune response.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attenuated">Attenuated</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Attenuated_vaccine" title="Attenuated vaccine">Attenuated vaccine</a></div>
<p>Some vaccines contain live, <a href="/wiki/Attenuated_vaccine" title="Attenuated vaccine">attenuated</a> microorganisms. Many of these are active <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viruses" title="Viruses">viruses</a> that have been cultivated under conditions that disable their virulent properties, or that use closely related but less dangerous organisms to produce a broad immune response. Although most attenuated vaccines are viral, some are bacterial in nature. Examples include the viral diseases <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mumps" title="Mumps">mumps</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rubella" title="Rubella">rubella</a>, and the bacterial disease <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typhoid" title="Typhoid">typhoid</a>. The live <i>Mycobacterium <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a></i> vaccine developed by Calmette and Guérin is not made of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">contagious</a> strain but contains a virulently modified strain called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacillus_Calmette-Gu%C3%A9rin" title="Bacillus Calmette-Guérin">BCG</a>" used to elicit an immune response to the vaccine. The live attenuated vaccine containing strain <i><a href="/wiki/Yersinia_pestis" title="Yersinia pestis">Yersinia pestis</a></i> EV is used for plague immunization. Attenuated vaccines have some advantages and disadvantages. Attenuated, or live, weakened, vaccines typically provoke more durable immunological responses. But they may not be safe for use in immunocompromised individuals, and on rare occasions mutate to a virulent form and cause disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhattacharya_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bhattacharya-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inactivated">Inactivated</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Inactivated_vaccine" title="Inactivated vaccine">Inactivated vaccine</a></div><p>
Some vaccines contain inactivated, but previously virulent, micro-organisms that have been destroyed with chemicals, heat, or radiation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> – "ghosts", with intact but empty bacterial cell envelopes. They are considered an intermediate phase between the inactivated and attenuated vaccines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BatahAly2020_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-BatahAly2020-56">[56]</a></sup> Examples include IPV (<a href="/wiki/Polio_vaccine" title="Polio vaccine">polio vaccine</a>), <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A_vaccine" title="Hepatitis A vaccine">hepatitis A vaccine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rabies_vaccine" title="Rabies vaccine">rabies vaccine</a> and most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Influenza_vaccines" title="Influenza vaccines">influenza vaccines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historyofvaccines.org_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-historyofvaccines.org-57">[57]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ReverseGeneticsFlu.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="491" decoding="async" height="367" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/ReverseGeneticsFlu.svg/300px-ReverseGeneticsFlu.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/ReverseGeneticsFlu.svg/450px-ReverseGeneticsFlu.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/ReverseGeneticsFlu.svg/600px-ReverseGeneticsFlu.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ReverseGeneticsFlu.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Avian_influenza" title="Avian influenza">Avian flu</a> vaccine development by <a href="/wiki/Reverse_genetics" title="Reverse genetics">reverse genetics</a> techniques</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxoid">Toxoid</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Toxoid" title="Toxoid">Toxoid</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Toxoid" title="Toxoid">Toxoid</a> vaccines are made from inactivated toxic compounds that cause illness rather than the micro-organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Examples of toxoid-based vaccines include <a href="/wiki/Tetanus" title="Tetanus">tetanus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diphtheria" title="Diphtheria">diphtheria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historyofvaccines.org_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-historyofvaccines.org-57">[57]</a></sup> Not all toxoids are for micro-organisms; for example, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crotalus_atrox" title="Crotalus atrox">Crotalus atrox</a></i> toxoid is used to vaccinate dogs against <a href="/wiki/Rattlesnake" title="Rattlesnake">rattlesnake</a> bites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subunit">Subunit</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Subunit_vaccine" title="Subunit vaccine">Subunit vaccine</a></div>
<p>Rather than introducing an inactivated or attenuated micro-organism to an immune system (which would constitute a "whole-agent" vaccine), a <a href="/wiki/Protein_subunit" title="Protein subunit">subunit</a> vaccine uses a fragment of it to create an immune response. One example is the subunit vaccine against <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B#Virology" title="Hepatitis B">hepatitis<span class="nowrap"> </span>B</a>, which is composed of only the surface proteins of the virus (previously extracted from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_serum" title="Blood serum">blood serum</a> of chronically infected patients but now produced by <a href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA" title="Recombinant DNA">recombination</a> of the viral genes into <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Another example is <a href="/wiki/Edible_algae_vaccine" title="Edible algae vaccine">edible algae vaccines</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Virus-like_particle" title="Virus-like particle">virus-like particle</a> (VLP) vaccine against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_papillomavirus" title="Human papillomavirus">human papillomavirus</a> (HPV), which is composed of the viral major <a href="/wiki/Capsid" title="Capsid">capsid</a> protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Another example is the <a href="/wiki/Hemagglutinin" title="Hemagglutinin">hemagglutinin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neuraminidase" title="Neuraminidase">neuraminidase</a> subunits of the <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a> virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historyofvaccines.org_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-historyofvaccines.org-57">[57]</a></sup> A subunit vaccine is being used for plague immunization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conjugate">Conjugate</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conjugate_vaccine" title="Conjugate vaccine">Conjugate vaccine</a></div>
<p>Certain bacteria have a <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharide</a> <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_capsule" title="Bacterial capsule">outer coat</a> that is poorly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunogenic" title="Immunogenic">immunogenic</a>. By linking these outer coats to proteins (e.g., toxins), the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> can be led to recognize the polysaccharide as if it were a protein antigen. This approach is used in the <a href="/wiki/Hib_vaccine" title="Hib vaccine"><i>Haemophilus influenzae</i> type B vaccine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outer_membrane_vesicle">Outer membrane vesicle</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bacterial_outer_membrane_vesicles" title="Bacterial outer membrane vesicles">Outer membrane vesicles</a> (OMVs) are naturally immunogenic and can be manipulated to produce potent vaccines. The best known OMV vaccines are those developed for <a href="/wiki/Meningococcal_vaccine#Serogroup_B" title="Meningococcal vaccine">serotype B meningococcal disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vaccinology-guide_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-vaccinology-guide-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heterotypic">Heterotypic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heterologous_vaccine" title="Heterologous vaccine">Heterologous vaccine</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Heterologous_vaccine" title="Heterologous vaccine">Heterologous vaccines</a> also known as "Jennerian vaccines", are vaccines that are pathogens of other animals that either do not cause disease or cause mild disease in the organism being treated. The classic example is Jenner's use of cowpox to protect against smallpox. A current example is the use of <a href="/wiki/BCG_vaccine" title="BCG vaccine">BCG vaccine</a> made from <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_bovis" title="Mycobacterium bovis">Mycobacterium bovis</a></i> to protect against <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_vaccine">Genetic vaccine</span></h3>
<p>The subgroup of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_vaccine" title="Genetic vaccine">genetic vaccines</a> encompass viral vector vaccines, RNA vaccines and DNA vaccines.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Viral_vector">Viral vector</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Viral_vector_vaccine" title="Viral vector vaccine">Viral vector vaccine</a></div>
<p>Viral vector vaccines use a safe <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">virus</a> to insert pathogen genes in the body to produce specific <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigens</a>, such as surface <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, to stimulate an <a href="/wiki/Immune_response" title="Immune response">immune response</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="RNA">RNA</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_vaccine" title="RNA vaccine">RNA vaccine</a></div>
<p>An mRNA vaccine (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_vaccine" title="RNA vaccine">RNA vaccine</a>) is a novel type of vaccine which is composed of the nucleic acid RNA, packaged within a vector such as lipid nanoparticles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Among the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_vaccine" title="COVID-19 vaccine">COVID-19 vaccines</a> are a number of RNA vaccines under development to combat the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a> and some have been approved or have received <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_use_authorization" title="Emergency use authorization">emergency use authorization</a> in some countries. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Pfizer%E2%80%93BioNTech_COVID-19_vaccine" title="Pfizer–BioNTech COVID-19 vaccine">Pfizer-BioNTech</a> vaccine and <a href="/wiki/Moderna_COVID-19_vaccine" title="Moderna COVID-19 vaccine">Moderna mRNA</a> vaccine are approved for use in adults (with Pfizer vaccine also fully approved for teens aged 16 to 17) in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="DNA">DNA</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/DNA_vaccine" title="DNA vaccine">DNA vaccine</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DNA_vaccination" title="DNA vaccination">DNA vaccination</a> – The proposed mechanism is the <a href="/wiki/Insertion_(genetics)" title="Insertion (genetics)">insertion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">expression</a> of viral or bacterial DNA in human or animal cells (enhanced by the use of <a href="/wiki/Electroporation" title="Electroporation">electroporation</a>), triggering immune system recognition. Some cells of the immune system that recognize the proteins expressed will mount an attack against these proteins and cells expressing them. Because these cells live for a very long time, if the <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogen</a> that normally expresses these proteins is encountered at a later time, they will be attacked instantly by the immune system. One potential advantage of DNA vaccines is that they are very easy to produce and store.
</p><p>In August 2021, Indian authorities gave emergency approval to <a href="/wiki/ZyCoV-D" title="ZyCoV-D">ZyCoV-D</a>. Developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadila_Healthcare" title="Cadila Healthcare">Cadila Healthcare</a>, it is the first DNA vaccine approved for humans.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Experimental">Experimental</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Agile_Pulse_In_Vivo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="989" data-file-width="925" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Agile_Pulse_In_Vivo.jpg/220px-Agile_Pulse_In_Vivo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Agile_Pulse_In_Vivo.jpg/330px-Agile_Pulse_In_Vivo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Agile_Pulse_In_Vivo.jpg/440px-Agile_Pulse_In_Vivo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Agile_Pulse_In_Vivo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Electroporation system for experimental "DNA vaccine" delivery</div></div></div>
<p>Many innovative vaccines are also in development and use.
</p>
<ul><li>Dendritic cell vaccines combine <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_cell" title="Dendritic cell">dendritic cells</a> with antigens to present the antigens to the body's white blood cells, thus stimulating an immune reaction. These vaccines have shown some positive preliminary results for treating brain tumors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> and are also tested in malignant melanoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24872109_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24872109-74">[74]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA" title="Recombinant DNA">Recombinant</a> <a href="/wiki/Viral_vector_vaccine" title="Viral vector vaccine">vector</a> – by combining the physiology of one micro-organism and the <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> of another, immunity can be created against diseases that have complex infection processes. An example is the <a href="/wiki/RVSV-ZEBOV_vaccine" title="RVSV-ZEBOV vaccine">RVSV-ZEBOV vaccine</a> licensed to Merck that is being used in 2018 to combat <a href="/wiki/2017_Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo_Ebola_virus_outbreak" title="2017 Democratic Republic of the Congo Ebola virus outbreak">ebola in Congo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/T-cell_receptor" title="T-cell receptor">T-cell receptor</a> peptide vaccines are under development for several diseases using models of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valley_Fever" title="Valley Fever">Valley Fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stomatitis" title="Stomatitis">stomatitis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Atopic_dermatitis" title="Atopic dermatitis">atopic dermatitis</a>. These peptides have been shown to modulate <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokine</a> production and improve cell-mediated immunity.</li>
<li>Targeting of identified bacterial proteins that are involved in complement inhibition would neutralize the key bacterial virulence mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19388175_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19388175-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>The use of <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmids</a> has been validated in preclinical studies as a protective vaccine strategy for cancer and infectious diseases. However, in human studies, this approach has failed to provide clinically relevant benefit. The overall efficacy of plasmid DNA immunization depends on increasing the plasmid's <a href="/wiki/Immunogenicity" title="Immunogenicity">immunogenicity</a> while also correcting for factors involved in the specific activation of immune effector cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe-77">[77]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">Bacterial vector</a> – Similar in principle to <a href="/wiki/Viral_vector_vaccine" title="Viral vector vaccine">viral vector vaccines</a>, but using bacteria instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vaccinology-guide_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-vaccinology-guide-64">[64]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Antigen-presenting_cell_vaccine" title="Antigen-presenting cell vaccine">Antigen-presenting cell</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vaccinology-guide_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-vaccinology-guide-64">[64]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>While most vaccines are created using inactivated or attenuated compounds from micro-organisms, <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_vaccine" title="Synthetic vaccine">synthetic vaccines</a> are composed mainly or wholly of synthetic peptides, carbohydrates, or antigens.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Valence">Valence</span></h2>
<p>Vaccines may be <i>monovalent</i> (also called <i>univalent</i>) or <i>multivalent</i> (also called <i>polyvalent</i>). A monovalent vaccine is designed to immunize against a single antigen or single microorganism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> A multivalent or polyvalent vaccine is designed to immunize against two or more strains of the same microorganism, or against two or more microorganisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The valency of a multivalent vaccine may be denoted with a Greek or Latin prefix (e.g., <i>tetravalent</i> or <i>quadrivalent</i>). In certain cases, a monovalent vaccine may be preferable for rapidly developing a strong immune response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>When two or more vaccines are mixed in the same formulation, the two vaccines can interfere. This most frequently occurs with live attenuated vaccines, where one of the vaccine components is more robust than the others and suppresses the growth and immune response to the other components. This phenomenon was first noted in the trivalent Sabin <a href="/wiki/Polio_vaccine" title="Polio vaccine">polio vaccine</a>, where the amount of serotype<span class="nowrap"> </span>2 virus in the vaccine had to be reduced to stop it from interfering with the "take" of the serotype<span class="nowrap"> </span>1 and<span class="nowrap"> </span>3 viruses in the vaccine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> This phenomenon has also been found to be a problem with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dengue" title="Dengue">dengue</a> vaccines currently being researched,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (July 2011)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> where the DEN-3 serotype was found to predominate and suppress the response to DEN-1, -2 and -4 serotypes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_contents">Other contents</span></h2>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Vaccine_ingredients" title="Vaccine ingredients">Vaccine ingredients</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Vaccine_ingredients&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:284px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topic_Two_Ingredients_static_8Dec2020.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2377" data-file-width="3961" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topic_Two_Ingredients_static_8Dec2020.jpg/282px-WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topic_Two_Ingredients_static_8Dec2020.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topic_Two_Ingredients_static_8Dec2020.jpg/423px-WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topic_Two_Ingredients_static_8Dec2020.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topic_Two_Ingredients_static_8Dec2020.jpg/564px-WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topic_Two_Ingredients_static_8Dec2020.jpg 2x" width="282"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WHO_EN_Vaccines_Topic_Two_Ingredients_static_8Dec2020.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graphic from the World Health Organization describing the main ingredients typically in vaccines.</div></div></div>
A vaccine dose contains many ingredients, very little of which is the active ingredient, the <a href="/wiki/Immunogen" title="Immunogen">immunogen</a>. A single dose may have merely nanograms of virus particles, or micrograms of bacterial polysaccharides. A vaccine injection, oral drops or nasal spray is mostly water. Other ingredients are added to boost the immune response, to ensure safety or help with storage, and a tiny amount of material is left-over from the manufacturing process. Very rarely, these materials can cause an allergic reaction in people who are very sensitive to them.</div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adjuvants">Adjuvants</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Immunologic_adjuvant" title="Immunologic adjuvant">Immunologic adjuvant</a></div>
<p>Vaccines typically contain one or more <a href="/wiki/Adjuvant" title="Adjuvant">adjuvants</a>, used to boost the immune response. Tetanus toxoid, for instance, is usually adsorbed onto <a href="/wiki/Alum" title="Alum">alum</a>. This presents the antigen in such a way as to produce a greater action than the simple aqueous tetanus toxoid. People who have an adverse reaction to adsorbed tetanus toxoid may be given the simple vaccine when the time comes for a booster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the preparation for the 1990 Persian Gulf campaign, the whole cell <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pertussis" title="Pertussis">pertussis</a> vaccine was used as an adjuvant for <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a> vaccine. This produces a more rapid immune response than giving only the anthrax vaccine, which is of some benefit if exposure might be imminent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preservatives">Preservatives</span></h3>
<p>Vaccines may also contain preservatives to prevent contamination with <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>. Until recent years, the preservative <a href="/wiki/Thiomersal" title="Thiomersal">thiomersal</a> (<abbr title="also known as">a.k.a.</abbr> <i>Thimerosal</i> in the US and Japan) was used in many vaccines that did not contain live viruses. As of 2005, the only childhood vaccine in the U.S. that contains thiomersal in greater than trace amounts is the influenza vaccine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> which is currently recommended only for children with certain risk factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Single-dose influenza vaccines supplied in the UK do not list thiomersal in the ingredients. Preservatives may be used at various stages of the production of vaccines, and the most sophisticated methods of measurement might detect traces of them in the finished product, as they may in the environment and population as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many vaccines need preservatives to prevent serious adverse effects such as <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus" title="Staphylococcus">Staphylococcus</a></i> infection, which in one 1928 incident killed 12 of 21 children inoculated with a <a href="/wiki/Diphtheria" title="Diphtheria">diphtheria</a> vaccine that lacked a preservative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Several preservatives are available, including thiomersal, <a href="/wiki/Phenoxyethanol" title="Phenoxyethanol">phenoxyethanol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>. Thiomersal is more effective against bacteria, has a better shelf-life, and improves vaccine stability, potency, and safety; but, in the U.S., the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, and a few other affluent countries, it is no longer used as a preservative in childhood vaccines, as a precautionary measure due to its <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Although <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thiomersal_controversy" title="Thiomersal controversy">controversial claims</a> have been made that thiomersal contributes to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autism_spectrum_disorder" title="Autism spectrum disorder">autism</a>, no convincing scientific evidence supports these claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Furthermore, a 10–11-year study of 657,461 children found that the MMR vaccine does not cause autism and actually reduced the risk of autism by seven percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Excipients">Excipients</span></h3>
<p>Beside the active vaccine itself, the following <a href="/wiki/Excipient" title="Excipient">excipients</a> and residual manufacturing compounds are present or may be present in vaccine preparations:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdc_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-cdc-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminum" title="Aluminum">Aluminum</a> salts or gels are added as <a href="/wiki/Adjuvant" title="Adjuvant">adjuvants</a>. Adjuvants are added to promote an earlier, more potent response, and more persistent immune response to the vaccine; they allow for a lower vaccine dosage.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">Antibiotics</a> are added to some vaccines to prevent the growth of bacteria during production and storage of the vaccine.</li>
<li>Egg <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> is present in the <a href="/wiki/Influenza_vaccine" title="Influenza vaccine">influenza vaccine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever_vaccine" title="Yellow fever vaccine">yellow fever vaccine</a> as they are prepared using chicken eggs. Other proteins may be present.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">Formaldehyde</a> is used to inactivate bacterial products for toxoid vaccines. Formaldehyde is also used to inactivate unwanted viruses and kill bacteria that might contaminate the vaccine during production.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Monosodium_glutamate" title="Monosodium glutamate">Monosodium glutamate</a> (MSG) and 2-<a href="/wiki/Phenoxyethanol" title="Phenoxyethanol">phenoxyethanol</a> are used as stabilizers in a few vaccines to help the vaccine remain unchanged when the vaccine is exposed to heat, light, acidity, or humidity.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thiomersal" title="Thiomersal">Thiomersal</a> is a mercury-containing antimicrobial that is added to vials of vaccines that contain more than one dose to prevent contamination and growth of potentially harmful bacteria. Due to the controversy surrounding thiomersal, it has been removed from most vaccines except multi-use influenza, where it was reduced to levels so that a single dose contained less than a microgram of mercury, a level similar to eating ten grams of canned tuna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDA_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FDA-94">[94]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span></h2>
<p>Various fairly standardized abbreviations for vaccine names have developed, although the standardization is by no means centralized or global. For example, the vaccine names used in the United States have well-established abbreviations that are also widely known and used elsewhere. An extensive list of them provided in a sortable table and freely accessible is available at a US <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> web page.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_US_vaccine_names_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_US_vaccine_names-95">[95]</a></sup> The page explains that "The abbreviations [in] this table (Column 3) were standardized jointly by staff of the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, <a href="/wiki/Advisory_Committee_on_Immunization_Practices" title="Advisory Committee on Immunization Practices">ACIP</a> Work Groups, the editor of the <i><a href="/wiki/Morbidity_and_Mortality_Weekly_Report" title="Morbidity and Mortality Weekly Report">Morbidity and Mortality Weekly Report</a></i> (MMWR), the editor of <i>Epidemiology and Prevention of Vaccine-Preventable Diseases</i> (the Pink Book), ACIP members, and liaison organizations to the ACIP."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_US_vaccine_names_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_US_vaccine_names-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some examples are "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DTaP" title="DTaP">DTaP</a>" for diphtheria and tetanus toxoids and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acellular" title="Acellular">acellular</a> pertussis vaccine, "DT" for diphtheria and tetanus toxoids, and "Td" for tetanus and diphtheria toxoids. At its page on tetanus vaccination,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_tetanus_vaccination_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_tetanus_vaccination-96">[96]</a></sup> the CDC further explains that "Upper-case letters in these abbreviations denote full-strength doses of diphtheria (D) and tetanus (T) toxoids and pertussis (P) vaccine. Lower-case "d" and "p" denote reduced doses of diphtheria and pertussis used in the adolescent/adult-formulations. The 'a' in DTaP and Tdap stands for 'acellular', meaning that the pertussis component contains only a part of the pertussis organism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_tetanus_vaccination_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_tetanus_vaccination-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another list of established vaccine abbreviations is at the CDC's page called "Vaccine Acronyms and Abbreviations", with abbreviations used on U.S. immunization records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_US_vaccine_abbreviations_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_US_vaccine_abbreviations-97">[97]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Adopted_Name" title="United States Adopted Name">United States Adopted Name</a> system has some conventions for the <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a> of vaccine names, placing <a href="/wiki/Head_(linguistics)" title="Head (linguistics)">head nouns</a> first and <a href="/wiki/Postpositive_adjective" title="Postpositive adjective">adjectives postpositively</a>. This is why the USAN for "<a href="/wiki/Polio_vaccine" title="Polio vaccine">OPV</a>" is "poliovirus vaccine live oral" rather than "oral poliovirus vaccine".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Licensing">Licensing</span></h2>
<p>A vaccine <i>licensure</i> occurs after the successful conclusion of the development cycle and further the clinical trials and other programs involved through <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_clinical_research" title="Phases of clinical research">Phases</a><span class="nowrap"> </span>I–III demonstrating safety, immunoactivity, immunogenetic safety at a given specific dose, proven effectiveness in preventing infection for target populations, and enduring preventive effect (time endurance or need for revaccination must be estimated).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who-vacc_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-who-vacc-98">[98]</a></sup> Because preventive vaccines are predominantly evaluated in healthy population cohorts and distributed among the general population, a high standard of safety is required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> As part of a multinational licensing of a vaccine, the World Health Organization <i>Expert Committee on Biological Standardization</i> developed guidelines of international standards for manufacturing and <a href="/wiki/Quality_control" title="Quality control">quality control</a> of vaccines, a process intended as a platform for national regulatory agencies to apply for their own licensing process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who-vacc_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-who-vacc-98">[98]</a></sup> Vaccine manufacturers do not receive licensing until a complete clinical cycle of development and trials proves the vaccine is safe and has long-term effectiveness, following scientific review by a multinational or national regulatory organization, such as the <a href="/wiki/European_Medicines_Agency" title="European Medicines Agency">European Medicines Agency</a> (EMA) or the US <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wijnans_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-wijnans-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chop_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-chop-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Upon <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a> adopting WHO guidelines for vaccine development and licensure, each country has its own responsibility to issue a national licensure, and to manage, deploy, and monitor the vaccine throughout its use in each nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who-vacc_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-who-vacc-98">[98]</a></sup> Building trust and acceptance of a licensed vaccine among the public is a task of communication by governments and healthcare personnel to ensure a vaccination campaign proceeds smoothly, saves lives, and enables economic recovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toner_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-toner-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> When a vaccine is licensed, it will initially be in limited supply due to variable manufacturing, distribution, and logistical factors, requiring an allocation plan for the limited supply and which population segments should be prioritized to first receive the vaccine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toner_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-toner-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Health_Organization">World Health Organization</span></h3>
<p>Vaccines developed for multinational distribution via the <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">United Nations Children's Fund (UNICEF)</a> require pre-qualification by the WHO to ensure <a href="/wiki/International_standard" title="International standard">international standards</a> of quality, safety, immunogenicity, and efficacy for adoption by numerous countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who-vacc_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-who-vacc-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>The process requires manufacturing consistency at WHO-contracted laboratories following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Good_Manufacturing_Practice" title="Good Manufacturing Practice">Good Manufacturing Practice</a> (GMP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who-vacc_98-4"><a href="#cite_note-who-vacc-98">[98]</a></sup> When UN agencies are involved in vaccine licensure, individual nations collaborate by 1) issuing marketing authorization and a national license for the vaccine, its manufacturers, and distribution partners; and 2) conducting <a href="/wiki/Postmarketing_surveillance" title="Postmarketing surveillance">postmarketing surveillance</a>, including records for adverse events after the vaccination program. The WHO works with national agencies to monitor inspections of manufacturing facilities and distributors for compliance with GMP and regulatory oversight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who-vacc_98-5"><a href="#cite_note-who-vacc-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some countries choose to buy vaccines licensed by reputable national organizations, such as EMA, FDA, or national agencies in other affluent countries, but such purchases typically are more expensive and may not have distribution resources suitable to local conditions in developing countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who-vacc_98-6"><a href="#cite_note-who-vacc-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span></h3>
<p>In the European Union (EU), vaccines for pandemic pathogens, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seasonal_influenza" title="Seasonal influenza">seasonal influenza</a>, are licensed EU-wide where all the <a href="/wiki/Member_state_of_the_European_Union" title="Member state of the European Union">member states</a> comply ("centralized"), are licensed for only some member states ("decentralized"), or are licensed on an individual national level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wijnans_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-wijnans-100">[100]</a></sup> Generally, all EU states follow regulatory guidance and clinical programs defined by the European <a href="/wiki/Committee_for_Medicinal_Products_for_Human_Use" title="Committee for Medicinal Products for Human Use">Committee for Medicinal Products for Human Use</a> (CHMP), a scientific panel of the <a href="/wiki/European_Medicines_Agency" title="European Medicines Agency">European Medicines Agency</a> (EMA) responsible for vaccine licensure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wijnans_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-wijnans-100">[100]</a></sup> The CHMP is supported by several expert groups who assess and monitor the progress of a vaccine before and after licensure and distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wijnans_100-3"><a href="#cite_note-wijnans-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h3>
<p>Under the FDA, the process of establishing evidence for vaccine clinical safety and efficacy is the same as for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_approval" title="Drug approval">the approval process for prescription drugs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-vacc_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-fda-vacc-104">[104]</a></sup> If successful through the stages of clinical development, the vaccine licensing process is followed by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biologics_License_Application" title="Biologics License Application">Biologics License Application</a> which must provide a scientific review team (from diverse disciplines, such as physicians, statisticians, microbiologists, chemists) and comprehensive documentation for the vaccine candidate having efficacy and safety throughout its development. Also during this stage, the proposed manufacturing facility is examined by expert reviewers for GMP compliance, and the label must have a compliant description to enable health care providers' definition of vaccine-specific use, including its possible risks, to communicate and deliver the vaccine to the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-vacc_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-fda-vacc-104">[104]</a></sup> After licensure, monitoring of the vaccine and its production, including periodic inspections for GMP compliance, continue as long as the manufacturer retains its license, which may include additional submissions to the FDA of tests for potency, safety, and purity for each vaccine manufacturing step.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-vacc_104-2"><a href="#cite_note-fda-vacc-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span></h3>
<p>Drugs Controller General of India is the head of department of the Central Drugs Standard Control Organization of the Government of India responsible for approval of licences of specified categories of drugs such as vaccines AND others like blood and blood products, IV fluids, and sera in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postmarketing_surveillance">Postmarketing surveillance</span></h3>
<p>Until a vaccine is in use for the general population, all potential <a href="/wiki/Vaccine_adverse_event" title="Vaccine adverse event">adverse events</a> from the vaccine may not be known, requiring manufacturers to conduct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phase_IV_trial" title="Phase IV trial">Phase<span class="nowrap"> </span>IV</a> studies for <a href="/wiki/Postmarketing_surveillance" title="Postmarketing surveillance">postmarketing surveillance</a> of the vaccine while it is used widely in the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who-vacc_98-7"><a href="#cite_note-who-vacc-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-vacc_104-3"><a href="#cite_note-fda-vacc-104">[104]</a></sup> The WHO works with UN member states to implement post-licensing surveillance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who-vacc_98-8"><a href="#cite_note-who-vacc-98">[98]</a></sup> The FDA relies on a <a href="/wiki/Vaccine_Adverse_Event_Reporting_System" title="Vaccine Adverse Event Reporting System">Vaccine Adverse Event Reporting System</a> to monitor safety concerns about a vaccine throughout its use in the American public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-vacc_104-4"><a href="#cite_note-fda-vacc-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scheduling">Scheduling</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vaccination_schedule" title="Vaccination schedule">Vaccination schedule</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For country-specific information on vaccination policies and practices, see <a href="/wiki/Vaccination_policy" title="Vaccination policy">Vaccination policy</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Share_of_children_who_receive_key_vaccines_in_target_populations,_OWID.svg"><img alt="Share of children who receive key vaccines in target populations, OWID.svg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Share_of_children_who_receive_key_vaccines_in_target_populations%2C_OWID.svg/350px-Share_of_children_who_receive_key_vaccines_in_target_populations%2C_OWID.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Share_of_children_who_receive_key_vaccines_in_target_populations%2C_OWID.svg/525px-Share_of_children_who_receive_key_vaccines_in_target_populations%2C_OWID.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Share_of_children_who_receive_key_vaccines_in_target_populations%2C_OWID.svg/700px-Share_of_children_who_receive_key_vaccines_in_target_populations%2C_OWID.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Share_of_children_who_receive_key_vaccines_in_target_populations,_OWID.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p>In order to provide the best protection, children are recommended to receive vaccinations as soon as their immune systems are sufficiently developed to respond to particular vaccines, with additional "booster" shots often required to achieve "full immunity". This has led to the development of complex vaccination schedules. Global recommendations of vaccination schedule are issued by <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Advisory_Group_of_Experts" title="Strategic Advisory Group of Experts">Strategic Advisory Group of Experts</a> and will be further translated by <a href="/wiki/National_Immunization_Technical_Advisory_Group" title="National Immunization Technical Advisory Group">advisory committee</a> at the country level with considering of local factors such as disease epidemiology, acceptability of vaccination, equity in local populations, and programmatic and financial constraint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Advisory_Committee_on_Immunization_Practices" title="Advisory Committee on Immunization Practices">Advisory Committee on Immunization Practices</a>, which recommends schedule additions for the <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a>, recommends routine vaccination of children against<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A" title="Hepatitis A">hepatitis A</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B_virus" title="Hepatitis B virus">hepatitis B</a>, polio, mumps, measles, rubella, <a href="/wiki/Diphtheria" title="Diphtheria">diphtheria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pertussis" title="Pertussis">pertussis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tetanus" title="Tetanus">tetanus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haemophilus_influenzae" title="Haemophilus influenzae">HiB</a>, chickenpox, <a href="/wiki/Rotavirus" title="Rotavirus">rotavirus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meningococcal_disease" title="Meningococcal disease">meningococcal disease</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>The large number of vaccines and boosters recommended (up to 24 injections by age two) has led to problems with achieving full compliance. To combat declining compliance rates, various notification systems have been instituted and many combination injections are now marketed (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Pentavalent_vaccine" title="Pentavalent vaccine">Pentavalent vaccine</a> and <a href="/wiki/MMRV_vaccine" title="MMRV vaccine">MMRV vaccine</a>), which protect against multiple diseases.
</p><p>Besides recommendations for infant vaccinations and boosters, many specific vaccines are recommended for other ages or for repeated injections throughout life – most commonly for measles, tetanus, influenza, and pneumonia. Pregnant women are often screened for continued resistance to rubella. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_papillomavirus" title="Human papillomavirus">human papillomavirus</a> vaccine is recommended in the U.S. (as of 2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and UK (as of 2009).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Vaccine recommendations for the elderly concentrate on pneumonia and influenza, which are more deadly to that group. In 2006, a vaccine was introduced against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herpes_zoster" title="Herpes zoster">shingles</a>, a disease caused by the chickenpox virus, which usually affects the elderly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zostavax_EPAR_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zostavax_EPAR-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scheduling and dosing of a vaccination may be tailored to the level of immunocompetence of an individual<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> and to optimize population-wide deployment of a vaccine when it supply is limited,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> e.g. in the setting of a pandemic.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics_of_development">Economics of development</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_vaccines" title="Economics of vaccines">Economics of vaccines</a></div>
<p>One challenge in vaccine development is economic: Many of the diseases most demanding a vaccine, including <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> and tuberculosis, exist principally in poor countries. Pharmaceutical firms and <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> companies have little incentive to develop vaccines for these diseases because there is little revenue potential. Even in more affluent countries, financial returns are usually minimal and the financial and other risks are great.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-market_return_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-market_return-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most vaccine development to date has relied on "push" funding by government, universities and non-profit organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Many vaccines have been highly cost effective and beneficial for <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The number of vaccines actually administered has risen dramatically in recent decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> This increase, particularly in the number of different vaccines administered to children before entry into schools may be due to government mandates and support, rather than economic incentive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Patents">Patents</span></h3>
<p>According to the World Health Organization, the biggest barrier to vaccine production in less developed countries has not been <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patents</a>, but the substantial financial, <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a>, and workforce requirements needed for market entry. Vaccines are complex mixtures of biological compounds, and unlike the case for <a href="/wiki/Prescription_drug" title="Prescription drug">prescription drugs</a>, there are no true <a href="/wiki/Generic_drug" title="Generic drug">generic vaccines</a>. The vaccine produced by a new facility must undergo complete clinical testing for safety and efficacy by the manufacturer. For most vaccines, specific processes in technology are patented. These can be circumvented by alternative manufacturing methods, but this required R&amp;D infrastructure and a suitably skilled workforce. In the case of a few relatively new vaccines, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_papillomavirus" title="Human papillomavirus">human papillomavirus</a> vaccine, the patents may impose an additional barrier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
