</p><p>When increased production of vaccines was urgently needed during the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a> in 2021, the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a> and governments around the world evaluated whether to waive <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property" title="Intellectual property">intellectual property</a> rights and patents on <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_vaccine" title="COVID-19 vaccine">COVID-19 vaccines</a>, which would "eliminate all potential barriers to the timely access of affordable COVID-19 medical products, including vaccines and medicines, and scale up the manufacturing and supply of essential medical products."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-somos_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-somos-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Preparation_of_measles_vaccines.jpg"><img alt="Preparation of measles vaccines.jpg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1095" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Preparation_of_measles_vaccines.jpg/220px-Preparation_of_measles_vaccines.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Preparation_of_measles_vaccines.jpg/330px-Preparation_of_measles_vaccines.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Preparation_of_measles_vaccines.jpg/440px-Preparation_of_measles_vaccines.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Preparation_of_measles_vaccines.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p>Vaccine production is fundamentally different from other kinds of manufacturing – including regular <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_manufacturing" title="Pharmaceutical manufacturing">pharmaceutical manufacturing</a> – in that vaccines are intended to be administered to millions of people of whom the vast majority are perfectly healthy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plotkin_Page_45_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plotkin_Page_45-121">[121]</a></sup> This fact drives an extraordinarily rigorous production process with strict compliance requirements that go far beyond what is required of other products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plotkin_Page_45_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plotkin_Page_45-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>Depending upon the antigen, it can cost anywhere from US$50 to $500 million to build a vaccine production facility, which requires highly specialized equipment, <a href="/wiki/Cleanroom" title="Cleanroom">clean rooms</a>, and containment rooms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plotkin_2017_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plotkin_2017-122">[122]</a></sup> There is a global scarcity of personnel with the right combination of skills, expertise, knowledge, competence and personality to staff vaccine production lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plotkin_2017_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plotkin_2017-122">[122]</a></sup> With the notable exceptions of Brazil, China, and India, many developing countries' educational systems are unable to provide enough qualified candidates, and vaccine makers based in such countries must hire expatriate personnel to keep production going.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plotkin_2017_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-Plotkin_2017-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vaccine production has several stages. First, the antigen itself is generated. Viruses are grown either on primary cells such as <a href="/wiki/Chicken_as_biological_research_model" title="Chicken as biological research model">chicken eggs</a> (e.g., for influenza) or on continuous cell lines such as cultured human cells (e.g., for <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A" title="Hepatitis A">hepatitis A</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Bacteria are grown in <a href="/wiki/Bioreactor" title="Bioreactor">bioreactors</a> (e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/Haemophilus_influenzae" title="Haemophilus influenzae">Haemophilus influenzae</a></i> type b). Likewise, a recombinant protein derived from the viruses or bacteria can be generated in yeast, bacteria, or cell cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOVaccSafety2_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOVaccSafety2-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the antigen is generated, it is isolated from the cells used to generate it. A virus may need to be inactivated, possibly with no further purification required. Recombinant proteins need many operations involving ultrafiltration and column chromatography. Finally, the vaccine is formulated by adding adjuvant, stabilizers, and preservatives as needed. The adjuvant enhances the immune response to the antigen, stabilizers increase the storage life, and preservatives allow the use of multidose vials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOVaccSafety2_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHOVaccSafety2-125">[125]</a></sup> Combination vaccines are harder to develop and produce, because of potential incompatibilities and interactions among the antigens and other ingredients involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bae_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bae-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>The final stage in vaccine manufacture before distribution is <a href="/wiki/Fill_and_finish" title="Fill and finish">fill and finish</a>, which is the process of filling vials with vaccines and packaging them for distribution. Although this is a conceptually simple part of the vaccine manufacture process, it is often a bottleneck in the process of distributing and administering vaccines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vaccine production techniques are evolving. Cultured <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mammalian_cells&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mammalian cells (page does not exist)">mammalian cells</a> are expected to become increasingly important, compared to conventional options such as chicken eggs, due to greater productivity and low incidence of problems with contamination. Recombination technology that produces genetically detoxified vaccines is expected to grow in popularity for the production of bacterial vaccines that use toxoids. Combination vaccines are expected to reduce the quantities of antigens they contain, and thereby decrease undesirable interactions, by using <a href="/wiki/Pathogen-associated_molecular_pattern" title="Pathogen-associated molecular pattern">pathogen-associated molecular patterns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bae_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bae-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccine_manufacturers">Vaccine manufacturers</span></h3>
<p>The companies with the highest market share in vaccine production are <a href="/wiki/Merck_%26_Co." title="Merck &amp; Co.">Merck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sanofi" title="Sanofi">Sanofi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GlaxoSmithKline" title="GlaxoSmithKline">GlaxoSmithKline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pfizer" title="Pfizer">Pfizer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Novartis" title="Novartis">Novartis</a>, with 70% of vaccine sales concentrated in the EU or US (2013).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plotkin2017_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-plotkin2017-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 42">: 42 </span></sup> Vaccine manufacturing plants require large capital investments ($50 million up to $300 million) and may take between 4 and 6 years to construct, with the full process of vaccine development taking between 10 and 15 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plotkin2017_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-plotkin2017-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 43">: 43 </span></sup> Manufacturing in developing countries is playing an increasing role in supplying these countries, specifically with regards to older vaccines and in Brazil, India and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plotkin2017_130-2"><a href="#cite_note-plotkin2017-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 47">: 47 </span></sup> The manufacturers in India are the most advanced in the developing world and include the <a href="/wiki/Serum_Institute_of_India" title="Serum Institute of India">Serum Institute of India</a>, one of the largest producers of vaccines by number of doses and an innovator in processes, recently improving efficiency of producing the measles vaccine by 10 to 20-fold, due to switching to a <a href="/wiki/MRC-5" title="MRC-5">MRC-5</a> cell culture instead of chicken eggs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plotkin2017_130-3"><a href="#cite_note-plotkin2017-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 48">: 48 </span></sup> China's manufacturing capabilities are focused on supplying their own domestic need, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinopharm" title="Sinopharm">Sinopharm (CNPGC)</a> alone providing over 85% of the doses for 14 different vaccines in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plotkin2017_130-4"><a href="#cite_note-plotkin2017-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 48">: 48 </span></sup> Brazil is approaching the point of supplying its own domestic needs using technology transferred from the developed world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plotkin2017_130-5"><a href="#cite_note-plotkin2017-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 49">: 49 </span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Delivery_systems">Delivery systems</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VaccineBySandraRugio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/VaccineBySandraRugio.jpg/220px-VaccineBySandraRugio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/VaccineBySandraRugio.jpg/330px-VaccineBySandraRugio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/VaccineBySandraRugio.jpg/440px-VaccineBySandraRugio.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VaccineBySandraRugio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A woman receiving a vaccine by injection</div></div></div>
<p>One of the most common methods of delivering vaccines into the human body is <a href="/wiki/Injection_(medicine)" title="Injection (medicine)">injection</a>.
</p><p>The development of new delivery systems raises the hope of vaccines that are safer and more efficient to deliver and administer. Lines of research include <a href="/wiki/Liposome" title="Liposome">liposomes</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/ISCOM" title="ISCOM">ISCOM</a></i> (immune stimulating complex).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Notable developments in vaccine delivery technologies have included oral vaccines. Early attempts to apply oral vaccines showed varying degrees of promise, beginning early in the 20th century, at a time when the very possibility of an effective oral antibacterial vaccine was controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> By the 1930s there was increasing interest in the prophylactic value of an oral <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a> vaccine for example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Research1929_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Research1929-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Polio_vaccine" title="Polio vaccine">oral polio vaccine</a> turned out to be effective when vaccinations were administered by volunteer staff without formal training; the results also demonstrated increased ease and efficiency of administering the vaccines. Effective oral vaccines have many advantages; for example, there is no risk of blood contamination. Vaccines intended for oral administration need not be liquid, and as solids, they commonly are more stable and less prone to damage or spoilage by freezing in transport and storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Such stability reduces the need for a "<a href="/wiki/Cold_chain" title="Cold chain">cold chain</a>": the resources required to keep vaccines within a restricted temperature range from the manufacturing stage to the point of administration, which, in turn, may decrease costs of vaccines.
</p><p>A microneedle approach, which is still in stages of development, uses "pointed projections fabricated into arrays that can create vaccine delivery pathways through the skin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>An experimental needle-free<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> vaccine delivery system is undergoing animal testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> A stamp-size patch similar to an <a href="/wiki/Adhesive_bandage" title="Adhesive bandage">adhesive bandage</a> contains about 20,000 microscopic projections per square cm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Dermis" title="Dermis">dermal</a> administration potentially increases the effectiveness of vaccination, while requiring less vaccine than injection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_veterinary_medicine">In veterinary medicine</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Influenza_vaccine#Flu_vaccine_for_nonhumans" title="Influenza vaccine">Influenza vaccine § Flu vaccine for nonhumans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vaccination_of_dogs" title="Vaccination of dogs">Vaccination of dogs</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_060815-N-0411D-018_U.S._Army_Veterinarian,_Capt_Gwynne_Kinley_of_Cape_Elizabeth,_Maine,_immunizes_a_goat_with_the_help_of_U.S._Navy_Operations_Specialist_2nd_Class_Jessica_Silva.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2316" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/US_Navy_060815-N-0411D-018_U.S._Army_Veterinarian%2C_Capt_Gwynne_Kinley_of_Cape_Elizabeth%2C_Maine%2C_immunizes_a_goat_with_the_help_of_U.S._Navy_Operations_Specialist_2nd_Class_Jessica_Silva.jpg/200px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/US_Navy_060815-N-0411D-018_U.S._Army_Veterinarian%2C_Capt_Gwynne_Kinley_of_Cape_Elizabeth%2C_Maine%2C_immunizes_a_goat_with_the_help_of_U.S._Navy_Operations_Specialist_2nd_Class_Jessica_Silva.jpg/300px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/US_Navy_060815-N-0411D-018_U.S._Army_Veterinarian%2C_Capt_Gwynne_Kinley_of_Cape_Elizabeth%2C_Maine%2C_immunizes_a_goat_with_the_help_of_U.S._Navy_Operations_Specialist_2nd_Class_Jessica_Silva.jpg/400px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_060815-N-0411D-018_U.S._Army_Veterinarian,_Capt_Gwynne_Kinley_of_Cape_Elizabeth,_Maine,_immunizes_a_goat_with_the_help_of_U.S._Navy_Operations_Specialist_2nd_Class_Jessica_Silva.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Goat vaccination against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheep_pox" title="Sheep pox">sheep pox</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pleural_pneumonia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pleural pneumonia (page does not exist)">pleural pneumonia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Vaccinations of animals are used both to prevent their contracting diseases and to prevent transmission of disease to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Both animals kept as pets and animals raised as livestock are routinely vaccinated. In some instances, wild populations may be vaccinated. This is sometimes accomplished with vaccine-laced food spread in a disease-prone area and has been used to attempt to control <a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a> in <a href="/wiki/Raccoon" title="Raccoon">raccoons</a>.
</p><p>Where rabies occurs, rabies vaccination of dogs may be required by law. Other canine vaccines include <a href="/wiki/Canine_distemper" title="Canine distemper">canine distemper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canine_parvovirus" title="Canine parvovirus">canine parvovirus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infectious_canine_hepatitis" title="Infectious canine hepatitis">infectious canine hepatitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adenoviridae#Animals" title="Adenoviridae">adenovirus-2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leptospirosis" title="Leptospirosis">leptospirosis</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Bordetella" title="Bordetella">Bordetella</a></i>, canine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parainfluenza_virus" title="Parainfluenza virus">parainfluenza virus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lyme_disease" title="Lyme disease">Lyme disease</a>, among others.
</p><p>Cases of veterinary vaccines used in humans have been documented, whether intentional or accidental, with some cases of resultant illness, most notably with <a href="/wiki/Brucellosis" title="Brucellosis">brucellosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-142">[142]</a></sup> However, the reporting of such cases is rare and very little has been studied about the safety and results of such practices. With the advent of aerosol vaccination in veterinary clinics, human exposure to pathogens not naturally carried in humans, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Bordetella_bronchiseptica" title="Bordetella bronchiseptica">Bordetella bronchiseptica</a></i>, has likely increased in recent years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-142">[142]</a></sup> In some cases, most notably <a href="/wiki/Rabies_vaccine" title="Rabies vaccine">rabies</a>, the parallel veterinary vaccine against a pathogen may be as much as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude" title="Orders of magnitude">orders of magnitude</a> more economical than the human one.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="DIVA_vaccines">DIVA vaccines</span></h3>
<p>DIVA (Differentiation of Infected from Vaccinated Animals), also known as SIVA (Segregation of Infected from Vaccinated Animals) vaccines, make it possible to differentiate between infected and vaccinated animals. DIVA vaccines carry at least one <a href="/wiki/Epitope" title="Epitope">epitope</a> less than the equivalent wild microorganism. An accompanying diagnostic test that detects the antibody against that epitope assists in identifying whether the animal has been vaccinated or not.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The first DIVA vaccines (formerly termed <a href="/wiki/Marker_vaccine" title="Marker vaccine">marker vaccines</a> and since 1999 coined as DIVA vaccines) and companion diagnostic tests were developed by J. T. van Oirschot and colleagues at the Central Veterinary Institute in Lelystad, The Netherlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-144">[144]</a></sup> They found that some existing vaccines against <a href="/wiki/Pseudorabies" title="Pseudorabies">pseudorabies</a> (also termed Aujeszky's disease) had deletions in their viral genome (among which was the gE gene). Monoclonal antibodies were produced against that deletion and selected to develop an <a href="/wiki/ELISA" title="ELISA">ELISA</a> that demonstrated antibodies against gE. In addition, novel genetically engineered gE-negative vaccines were constructed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Along the same lines, DIVA vaccines and companion diagnostic tests against bovine herpesvirus<span class="nowrap"> </span>1 infections have been developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>The DIVA strategy has been applied in various countries to successfully eradicate pseudorabies virus from those countries. Swine populations were intensively vaccinated and monitored by the companion diagnostic test and, subsequently, the infected pigs were removed from the population. Bovine herpesvirus<span class="nowrap"> </span>1 DIVA vaccines are also widely used in practice.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Considerable efforts are ongoing to apply the DIVA principle to a wide range of infectious diseases, such as classical swine fever,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> avian influenza,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12745380_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12745380-148">[148]</a></sup> <i>Actinobacillus pleuropneumonia</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> and <i>Salmonella</i> infections in pigs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inoculation_day_16.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="462" data-file-width="743" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Inoculation_day_16.png/220px-Inoculation_day_16.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Inoculation_day_16.png/330px-Inoculation_day_16.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Inoculation_day_16.png/440px-Inoculation_day_16.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inoculation_day_16.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Cowpox" title="Cowpox">cowpox</a> <a href="/wiki/Inoculation" title="Inoculation">inoculations</a> sixteen days after administration (1802)</div></div></div>
<p>Prior to the introduction of vaccination with material from cases of cowpox (heterotypic immunisation), smallpox could be prevented by deliberate <a href="/wiki/Variolation" title="Variolation">variolation</a> with smallpox virus. The earliest hints of the practice of variolation for smallpox in China come during the tenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_6_part_6_154_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_6_part_6_154-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (January 2022)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Chinese also practiced the oldest documented use of variolation, dating back to the fifteenth century. They implemented a method of "nasal <a href="/wiki/Insufflation_(medicine)" title="Insufflation (medicine)">insufflation</a>" administered by blowing powdered smallpox material, usually scabs, up the nostrils. Various insufflation techniques have been recorded throughout the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries within China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2010_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2010-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 60">: 60 </span></sup> Two reports on the Chinese practice of <a href="/wiki/Inoculation" title="Inoculation">inoculation</a> were received by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> in London in 1700; one by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Lister" title="Martin Lister">Martin Lister</a> who received a report by an employee of the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> stationed in China and another by <a href="/wiki/Clopton_Havers" title="Clopton Havers">Clopton Havers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jenner_and_his_two_colleagues_seeing_off_three_anti-vaccinat_Wellcome_V0011075.jpg"><img alt="Jenner and his two colleagues seeing off three anti-vaccinat Wellcome V0011075.jpg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2324" data-file-width="3372" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Jenner_and_his_two_colleagues_seeing_off_three_anti-vaccinat_Wellcome_V0011075.jpg/220px-Jenner_and_his_two_colleagues_seeing_off_three_anti-vaccinat_Wellcome_V0011075.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Jenner_and_his_two_colleagues_seeing_off_three_anti-vaccinat_Wellcome_V0011075.jpg/330px-Jenner_and_his_two_colleagues_seeing_off_three_anti-vaccinat_Wellcome_V0011075.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Jenner_and_his_two_colleagues_seeing_off_three_anti-vaccinat_Wellcome_V0011075.jpg/440px-Jenner_and_his_two_colleagues_seeing_off_three_anti-vaccinat_Wellcome_V0011075.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jenner_and_his_two_colleagues_seeing_off_three_anti-vaccinat_Wellcome_V0011075.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary_Wortley_Montagu" title="Mary Wortley Montagu">Mary Wortley Montagu</a>, who had witnessed variolation in Turkey, had her four-year-old daughter variolated in the presence of physicians of the Royal Court in 1721 upon her return to England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2010_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2010-152">[152]</a></sup> Later on that year, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Maitland_(physician)" title="Charles Maitland (physician)">Charles Maitland</a> conducted an experimental variolation of six prisoners in <a href="/wiki/Newgate_Prison" title="Newgate Prison">Newgate Prison</a> in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenner1988_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-fenner1988-154">[154]</a></sup> The experiment was a success, and soon variolation was drawing attention from the royal family, who helped promote the procedure. However, in 1783, several days after <a href="/wiki/Prince_Octavius_of_Great_Britain" title="Prince Octavius of Great Britain">Prince Octavius of Great Britain</a> was inoculated, he died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baxby_1984_303–07_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baxby_1984_303–07-155">[155]</a></sup> In 1796, the <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Jenner" title="Edward Jenner">Edward Jenner</a> took pus from the hand of a milkmaid with <a href="/wiki/Cowpox" title="Cowpox">cowpox</a>, scratched it into the arm of an 8-year-old boy, <a href="/wiki/James_Phipps" title="James Phipps">James Phipps</a>, and six weeks later variolated the boy with smallpox, afterwards observing that he did not catch smallpox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern-Markel_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern-Markel-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunn-157">[157]</a></sup> Jenner extended his studies and, in 1798, reported that his vaccine was safe in children and adults, and could be transferred from arm-to-arm, which reduced reliance on uncertain supplies from infected cows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baxby_1984_303–07_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baxby_1984_303–07-155">[155]</a></sup> In 1804, the Spanish <a href="/wiki/Balmis_Expedition" title="Balmis Expedition">Balmis smallpox vaccination expedition</a> to Spain's colonies Mexico and Philippines used the arm-to-arm transport method to get around the fact the vaccine survived for only 12 days <i><a href="/wiki/In_vitro" title="In vitro">in vitro</a></i>. They used cowpox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Since vaccination with cowpox was much safer than smallpox inoculation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> the latter, though still widely practiced in England, was banned in 1840.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Centenaire_de_la_d%C3%A9couverte_de_la_vaccine_par_Jenner_CIPB0429.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1662" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Centenaire_de_la_d%C3%A9couverte_de_la_vaccine_par_Jenner_CIPB0429.jpg/220px-Centenaire_de_la_d%C3%A9couverte_de_la_vaccine_par_Jenner_CIPB0429.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Centenaire_de_la_d%C3%A9couverte_de_la_vaccine_par_Jenner_CIPB0429.jpg/330px-Centenaire_de_la_d%C3%A9couverte_de_la_vaccine_par_Jenner_CIPB0429.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Centenaire_de_la_d%C3%A9couverte_de_la_vaccine_par_Jenner_CIPB0429.jpg/440px-Centenaire_de_la_d%C3%A9couverte_de_la_vaccine_par_Jenner_CIPB0429.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Centenaire_de_la_d%C3%A9couverte_de_la_vaccine_par_Jenner_CIPB0429.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>French print in 1896 marking the centenary of Jenner's vaccine</div></div></div>
<p>Following on from Jenner's work, the second generation of vaccines was introduced in the 1880s by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> who developed vaccines for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicken_cholera" title="Chicken cholera">chicken cholera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pasteur1881_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pasteur1881-15">[15]</a></sup> and from the late nineteenth century vaccines were considered a matter of national prestige. National <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vaccination_policies" title="Vaccination policies">vaccination policies</a> were adopted and compulsory vaccination laws were passed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern-Markel_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stern-Markel-156">[156]</a></sup> In 1931 <a href="/wiki/Alice_Miles_Woodruff" title="Alice Miles Woodruff">Alice Miles Woodruff</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ernest_Goodpasture" title="Ernest Goodpasture">Ernest Goodpasture</a> documented that the <a href="/wiki/Fowlpox" title="Fowlpox">fowlpox</a> virus could be grown in <a href="/wiki/Embryonated" title="Embryonated">embryonated</a> chicken <a href="/wiki/Egg" title="Egg">egg</a>. Soon scientists began cultivating other viruses in eggs. Eggs were used for virus propagation in the development of a <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever_vaccine" title="Yellow fever vaccine">yellow fever vaccine</a> in 1935 and an <a href="/wiki/Influenza_vaccine" title="Influenza vaccine">influenza vaccine</a> in 1945. In 1959 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Growth_media" title="Growth media">growth media</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cell_culture" title="Cell culture">cell culture</a> replaced eggs as the standard method of virus propagation for vaccines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vaccinology flourished in the twentieth century, which saw the introduction of several successful vaccines, including those against <a href="/wiki/Diphtheria" title="Diphtheria">diphtheria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mumps" title="Mumps">mumps</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rubella" title="Rubella">rubella</a>. Major achievements included the development of the <a href="/wiki/Polio_vaccine" title="Polio vaccine">polio vaccine</a> in the 1950s and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eradication_of_smallpox" title="Eradication of smallpox">eradication of smallpox</a> during the 1960s and 1970s. <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Hilleman" title="Maurice Hilleman">Maurice Hilleman</a> was the most prolific of the developers of the vaccines in the twentieth century. As vaccines became more common, many people began taking them for granted. However, vaccines remain elusive for many important diseases, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herpes" title="Herpes">herpes simplex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gonorrhea" title="Gonorrhea">gonorrhea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern-Markel_156-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stern-Markel-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BaardaSikora2015_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-BaardaSikora2015-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Generations_of_vaccines">Generations of vaccines</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Smallpox_and_anthrax_vaccines_of_447th_Expeditionary_Medical_Squadron.jpg"><img alt="Smallpox and anthrax vaccines of 447th Expeditionary Medical Squadron.jpg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Smallpox_and_anthrax_vaccines_of_447th_Expeditionary_Medical_Squadron.jpg/220px-Smallpox_and_anthrax_vaccines_of_447th_Expeditionary_Medical_Squadron.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Smallpox_and_anthrax_vaccines_of_447th_Expeditionary_Medical_Squadron.jpg/330px-Smallpox_and_anthrax_vaccines_of_447th_Expeditionary_Medical_Squadron.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Smallpox_and_anthrax_vaccines_of_447th_Expeditionary_Medical_Squadron.jpg/440px-Smallpox_and_anthrax_vaccines_of_447th_Expeditionary_Medical_Squadron.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Smallpox_and_anthrax_vaccines_of_447th_Expeditionary_Medical_Squadron.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p>First generation vaccines are whole-organism vaccines – either live and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attenuated_virus" title="Attenuated virus">weakened</a>, or killed forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alarcon1999_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alarcon1999-163">[163]</a></sup> Live, attenuated vaccines, such as smallpox and polio vaccines, are able to induce <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxic_T_cell" title="Cytotoxic T cell">killer T-cell</a> (T<sub>C</sub> or CTL) responses, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helper_T_cell" title="Helper T cell">helper T-cell</a> (T<sub>H</sub>) responses and <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibody</a> <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immunity</a>. However, attenuated forms of a <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogen</a> can convert to a dangerous form and may cause disease in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunocompromised" title="Immunocompromised">immunocompromised</a> vaccine recipients (such as those with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIDS" title="AIDS">AIDS</a>). While killed vaccines do not have this risk, they cannot generate specific killer T cell responses and may not work at all for some diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alarcon1999_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alarcon1999-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>Second generation vaccines were developed to reduce the risks from live vaccines. These are subunit vaccines, consisting of specific <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigens</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Tetanus" title="Tetanus">tetanus</a> or <a href="/wiki/Diphtheria" title="Diphtheria">diphtheria</a> <a href="/wiki/Toxoid" title="Toxoid">toxoid</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA" title="Recombinant DNA">recombinant</a> protein components (such as the hepatitis B surface antigen). They can generate T<sub>H</sub> and antibody responses, but not killer T cell responses.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_vaccine" title="RNA vaccine">RNA vaccines</a> and <a href="/wiki/DNA_vaccine" title="DNA vaccine">DNA vaccines</a> are examples of third generation vaccines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alarcon1999_163-2"><a href="#cite_note-Alarcon1999-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson2000_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson2000-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> In 2016 a DNA vaccine for the <a href="/wiki/Zika_virus" title="Zika virus">Zika virus</a> began testing at the <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health</a>. Separately, Inovio Pharmaceuticals and GeneOne Life Science began tests of a different DNA vaccine against Zika in Miami. Manufacturing the vaccines in volume was unsolved as of 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> Clinical trials for DNA vaccines to prevent HIV are underway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRNA_vaccines" title="MRNA vaccines">mRNA vaccines</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BNT162b2" title="BNT162b2">BNT162b2</a> were developed in the year 2020 with the help of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Warp_Speed" title="Operation Warp Speed">Operation Warp Speed</a> and massively deployed to combat the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a>. In 2021, <a href="/wiki/Katalin_Karik%C3%B3" title="Katalin Karikó">Katalin Karikó</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drew_Weissman" title="Drew Weissman">Drew Weissman</a> received Columbia University's Horwitz Prize for their pioneering research in mRNA vaccine technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trends">Trends</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px 0;overflow:hidden;width:238px;border-collapse:collapse;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em}.mw-parser-output .ambox-speedy{border-left:10px solid #b32424;background-color:#fee7e6}.mw-parser-output .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="Ambox current red Americas.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2018</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Since at least 2013, scientists have been trying to develop synthetic third-generation vaccines by reconstructing the outside structure of a <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">virus</a>; it was hoped that this will help prevent <a href="/wiki/Vaccine_resistance" title="Vaccine resistance">vaccine resistance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>Principles that govern the immune response can now be used in tailor-made vaccines against many noninfectious human diseases, such as cancers and autoimmune disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> For example, the experimental vaccine <a href="/wiki/CYT006-AngQb" title="CYT006-AngQb">CYT006-AngQb</a> has been investigated as a possible treatment for <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">high blood pressure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Factors that affect the trends of vaccine development include progress in translatory medicine, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demographics" title="Demographics">demographics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regulatory_Science" title="Regulatory Science">regulatory science</a>, political, cultural, and social responses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants_as_bioreactors_for_vaccine_production">Plants as bioreactors for vaccine production</span></h3>
<p>The idea of vaccine production via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transgenic_plants" title="Transgenic plants">transgenic plants</a> was identified as early as 2003. Plants such as <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomato</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">banana</a> can have genes inserted that cause them to produce vaccines usable for humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12531364_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12531364-173">[173]</a></sup> In 2005, bananas were developed that produce a human vaccine against <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B" title="Hepatitis B">hepatitis B</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccine_hesitancy">Vaccine hesitancy</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vaccine_hesitancy" title="Vaccine hesitancy">Vaccine hesitancy</a> is a delay in acceptance, or refusal of vaccines despite the availability of vaccine services. The term covers outright refusals to vaccinate, delaying vaccines, accepting vaccines but remaining uncertain about their use, or using certain vaccines but not others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2015_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2015-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larson2014_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larson2014-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> There is an overwhelming <a href="/wiki/Scientific_consensus" title="Scientific consensus">scientific consensus</a> that vaccines are generally safe and effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> Vaccine hesitancy often results in disease <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outbreak" title="Outbreak">outbreaks</a> and deaths from <a href="/wiki/Vaccine-preventable_diseases" title="Vaccine-preventable diseases">vaccine-preventable diseases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfesharp_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-wolfesharp-185">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AgeOld_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-AgeOld-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Therefore, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> characterizes vaccine hesitancy as one of the top ten global health threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1093669538">.mw-parser-output .portalbox{padding:0}.mw-parser-output .portalborder{border:solid #aaa 1px}.mw-parser-output .portalbox.tleft{margin:0.5em 1em 0.5em 0}.mw-parser-output .portalbox.tright{margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul{display:table;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:175px;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .portalborder>ul{padding:0.1em;background:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li{display:table-row}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li>span:first-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li>span:last-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle}</style><div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint plainlist portalbox portalborder tright" role="navigation">
